   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d132592373" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Recently, a high-entropy strategy has attracted extensive attention and is applied to the preparation of electrode materials for energy storage batteries, aiming to improve electrochemical performance. It is found that adjusting the conformational entropy of the material can significantly enhance ion and electron transport efficiency, as well as improve the structural stability of the host material. However, there still remains a lack of deep understanding into the high-entropy strategy, specifically regarding how this approach can alter the intrinsic properties of the material. In this work, the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is designed and prepared as a model material with higher entropy, and ultimately, an optimized sample of Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.6</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) is achieved. The results indicate that increasing the entropy value of the material notably enhances its crystal structure, diffusion kinetics, and interfacial stability. Consequently, this optimized sample demonstrates deep insertion/extraction of 2.8 Na<sup>+</sup>, yielding an impressively high capacity of 122.3 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.1C, alongside an ultra-high rate capability of 100C. Remarkably, it also sustains performance over 14000 cycles at 50C. This study underscores a method for fabricating high-performance electrode materials through the implementation of the entropy strategy.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202412730-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Renewable energy sources, including wind and solar power, are recognized as pivotal in promoting a sustainable economic model. However, their inherent intermittency and randomness hinder their practical application to some extent.</p>
         
         <p>To mitigate this issue, there is an urgent demand large-scale electrochemical energy storage systems (EESSs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0001_R_d132592361e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered the optimal choice for EESSs due to their abundant sodium resources, lower costs, and higher safety features.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0002_R_d132592361e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev. Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cathode materials, serving as an integral component of SIBs, are presently the subject of significant research interest, with emphasis placed on layered oxides, Prussian blue analogs, and polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0003_R_d132592361e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these, polyanionic compounds stand out for their superior cycling stability, high theoretical capacity, and elevated operating voltage. Particularly, an iron-based mixed phosphate, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NFPP), is highlighted as a highly promising cathode material for SIBs, owing to its distinctive three-dimensional (3D) open architecture, considerable operating potential (3.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), significant theoretical capacity (1C = 129 mAh g<sup>1</sup>), and a low volume change rate (4%).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0004_R_d132592361e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, the electrochemical performance of NFPP is somewhat constrained due to its inferior electronic conductivity and sluggish sodium-ion diffusion behavior, particularly in terms of rate capability and cyclic stability, which require enhancement. To address these issues, various strategies such as nanosizing, carbon coating (e.g., rGO, CNT), and elemental doping (e.g., Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>) are frequently employed optimization methods.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0005_R_d132592361e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Shen et&nbsp;al. developed a mesoporous NFPP structure with a core-double-shell architecture, creating a fast 3D electron-conducting network that enhances both cycling stability and rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0006_R_d132592361e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Eng. J.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, Mai et&nbsp;al. have shown that Mg-doped NFPP/C cathodes introduced pseudocapacitance, thereby improving electrochemical kinetics and resulting in superior rate performance and significantly extended cycle life compared to traditional faradaic reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0007_R_d132592361e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct Materials.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, Deng et&nbsp;al. found that NFPP doped with 5% manganese not only improved rate performance but also supported operation across a broad temperature range (40 to 60&nbsp;C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0008_R_d132592361e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the present conductivity levels and capacity release are deemed insufficient, and it is generally considered that both nano-materialization and carbon coating aim to shorten the ion diffusion path or improve the conductivity of the material from the outside. Improving the electrochemical performance of the electrode materials by changing their intrinsic structural characteristics remains a significant challenge and an urgent desire in this field.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, a strategy known as high-entropy (HE) modification of multiple elements/components has yielded significant achievements in fields such as catalysis, electrolyte, and electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0009_R_d132592361e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Joule. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Sustain. Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A value of configurational entropy (S<sub>config</sub>) serves as a quantitative standard of the systems' entropy values.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0010_R_d132592361e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simply put, the S<sub>config</sub> of a system is predominantly manipulated by choosing an array of cations or anions that possess similar ionic radius but exhibit distinct characteristics. This methodology is well known due to its cocktail effect, a synergistic effect that often leads to unexpected results. For example, Zhang et&nbsp;al. employed high-entropy doping in a cobalt-free, high-nickel (80% Ni) cathode material system (HE-LNMO) through a conventional co-precipitation method, achieving a notable zero strain (volume change rate less than 0.5%).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0011_R_d132592361e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nature">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, Hu et&nbsp;al. proposed a HE strategy to boost the overall energy density and structural integrity of Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.07</sub>Cu<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.11</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.41</sub>O<sub>1.97</sub>F<sub>0.03</sub> through the substitution of anions and cations, thereby enabling high-voltage transition metal (TM) redox activities and reducing manganese Jahn-Teller distortion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0012_R_d132592361e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even in the polyanionic compounds, Zhang et&nbsp;al. synthesized the HE NASICON cathode Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>V<sub>0.4</sub>Cr<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, confirming that the HE effect could mitigate structural degradation during electrochemical cycling at high voltages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0013_R_d132592361e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, Zhao et&nbsp;al. demonstrated that Na<sub>3</sub>VAl<sub>0.2</sub>Cr<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>In<sub>0.2</sub>Ga<sub>0.2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> achieved continuous oxidation of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> while maintaining excellent cycling and rate capabilities across a wide temperature range.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0014_R_d132592361e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The above findings suggest that electrode materials optimized through entropy strategies manifest unique energy storage characteristics, including zero strain, stable high-voltage structures, and the activation of multi-electron reactions. This advancement offers fresh insights into the development of cathode materials for SIBs. In fact, not only high-entropy but also medium-entropy (ME) strategies are effective in the design of advanced NASICON-type cathodes, showing notable improvement in kinetic properties and stable crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0015_R_d132592361e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specific to NFPP, since its crystal structure already contains complex [PO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>3</sup> and [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>]<sup>4</sup> anions, could this entropy strategy be effective for such complex anionic materials? In what aspects might it exhibit a cocktail effect?</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we designed and synthesized a series of NFPPs with different S<sub>config</sub> using the sol-gel method, with the following general formula Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.9-x-y-z</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>y</sub>Mg<sub>z</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>): when x = 0.3, y = 0.3, z = 0.3, i.e., Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.0</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (denoted as MCM333, 1C = 122 mAh g<sup>1</sup>); when x = 0.2, y = 0.3, z = 0.3, i.e., Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.1</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Cu<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (denoted as MCM233, 1C = 118mAh g<sup>1</sup>); when x = 0.1, y = 0.3, z = 0.3, i.e., Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.2</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Cu<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (denoted as MCM133, 1C = 114mAh g<sup>1</sup>); and when x = 0.1, y = 0.1, z = 0.1, i.e., Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.6</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (denoted as MCM111, 1C = 129 mAh g<sup>1</sup>). Notably, MCM111 exhibited outstanding electrochemical performance, primarily attributed to its optimized crystal structure, which significantly improved conductivity and sodium ion diffusion kinetics. Finally, through a variety of characterizations and theoretical analyses, a comprehensive study was conducted on potential plateaus, kinetic behavior, structural evolution, charge compensation, and interfacial stability, among other factors. Additionally, we examined the synergistic interaction among various elements, providing a novel insight into understanding the cocktail effect more effectively.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202412730-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The concept of HE first appeared in the field of alloys and was introduced in 2004 by Yeh et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0016_R_d132592361e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Eng. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, in 2015, it was introduced to rock-salt oxides, which contain both cations and anions, a property similar to polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0017_R_d132592361e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun. Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on previous studies, the S<sub>config</sub> equation involving anions and cations can be summarized according to statistical thermodynamics as follows:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202412730-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8b2a558b-9827-414d-8ba4-ec179286619d/adfm202412730-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202412730:adfm202412730-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202412730-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal"></mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>S</mi>
                              <mi>config</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak"></mo>
                           <mi>R</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo stretchy="true">[</mo>
                              <mo stretchy="true">(</mo>
                           </mrow>
                           <munderover>
                              <mo></mo>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                                 <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                 <mo>=</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </mrow>
                              <mi>N</mi>
                           </munderover>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>x</mi>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                              <mi>ln</mi>
                              <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>x</mi>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo stretchy="true">)</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                              <mo stretchy="true">(</mo>
                              <munderover>
                                 <mo></mo>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>j</mi>
                                    <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                    <mo>=</mo>
                                    <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mi>M</mi>
                              </munderover>
                              <mrow>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>J</mi>
                                    <mi>i</mi>
                                 </msub>
                                 <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                 <mi>ln</mi>
                                 <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>J</mi>
                                    <mi>i</mi>
                                 </msub>
                                 <mo stretchy="true">)</mo>
                                 <mo stretchy="true">]</mo>
                              </mrow>
                           </mrow>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\Delta {{S}_{{\mathrm{config}}}}\; = \; - R {\bigg [ \bigg(}\sum\limits_{i\; = \;1}^N {{{x}_i}\;\ln \;{{x}_i}\bigg )\; + \;\bigg(\sum\limits_{j\; = \;1}^M {{{J}_i}\;\ln \;{{J}_i}\bigg)\bigg]} } \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>M</i> and <i>N</i> represent the number of cationic and anionic elemental species, respectively; <i>x<sub>i</sub></i> and <i>j<sub>i</sub></i> denote the mole fractions of ions at the cationic and anionic sites, respectively; and <i>R</i> is the universal gas constant. It is noteworthy that S<sub>config</sub> reaches its maximum value when the mole fraction of each element at the substituted site is the same. A system with S<sub>config</sub>  1.5R is generally considered to be of HE, between 1.0R and 1.5R is of ME, and below 1.0R is of LE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0015_R_d132592361e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0018_R_d132592361e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Z. Zhang, Small.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
         
         <p>It is noticeable that current studies mainly focus on LE and ME, since HE is primarily synthesized through time-consuming trial-and-error methods, thus limiting the relevant research.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0019_R_d132592361e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1a</a>, MCM333 and MCM233 belong to ME, while MCM133 and MCM111 are LE. It should be noted, however, that even the low-entropy MCM133 and MCM111 can still be considered a high-entropy material compared to the pure NFPP.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202412730-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/019954b2-904f-4441-8157-4db321d76f3b/adfm202412730-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/019954b2-904f-4441-8157-4db321d76f3b/adfm202412730-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a79d8e93-b5aa-40d0-8e28-421e0148f573/adfm202412730-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/019954b2-904f-4441-8157-4db321d76f3b/adfm202412730-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202412730-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202412730" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Conformational entropy comparison between this study and references; b,c) X-ray diffraction patterns and its local magnification; d) rietveld refinement of MCM111; e) MCM11 HRTEM image and carbon layer schematic; f) Typical STEM-EDS elemental mappings of MCM111;g) comparison of theoretical elemental content with actual ICP results.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202412730-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural and Morphological Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Here, a series of materials with different S<sub>config</sub> were designed and synthesized, and their X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1b</a>. It is evident that all entropy strategy-optimized (ESO) samples exhibit the same XRD patterns as NFPP, aligning well with previously reported NFPP diffraction data.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0007_R_d132592361e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct Materials.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggests that the materials have the same crystal structure (orthorhombic space group), all belonging to <i>Pn2<sub>1</sub>a</i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0020_R_d132592361e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a close examination of the diffraction peaks in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1c</a> reveals that the ESO samples exhibit a slight shift toward lower angles, indicating an increase in lattice spacing, which is most likely ascribed to the differences in ionic radius among the doping elements (Mn<sup>2+</sup>, 0.80 ; V<sup>3+</sup>, 0.74 ; Cu<sup>2+</sup>, 0.72 ; Mg<sup>2+</sup>, 0.65 ) relative to Fe<sup>2+</sup> (0.76 ). It is worth mentioning that the degree of peak shift in MCM samples with different manganese contents is almost the same. This may be due to varying degrees of lattice distortion caused by the high entropy strategy, which is consistent with those observed in other high entropy materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0021_R_d132592361e1030" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Nano">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>For simplicity, a preliminary electrochemical test was conducted on all the materials, and their charge/discharge curves between 1.54.2&nbsp;V at 1C are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The MCM111 sample exhibited the best performance with the highest specific reversible capacity; therefore, this sample was selected as the high-entropy model material for the subsequent series of characterizations and tests. Rietveld refinement analysis was first performed to investigate their crystal structure (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) and lattice parameters (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) of MCM111and NFPP. Compared to NFPP, MCM111 exhibited a slight reduction along the <i>a</i>-axis and an increase along the <i>b</i>- and <i>c</i>-axes, accompanied by an expansion in the cell volume, which may widen the ion diffusion pathways and enhance the kinetic behavior of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0022_R_d132592361e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was also employed to characterize the structure in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), with the findings coinciding well with the previous reports on NFPP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0008_R_d132592361e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The micro-morphology of MCM111 and NFPP were elucidated through scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). In contrast to the large bulk of NFPP, MCM111 was mainly composed of irregular particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis indicates that the carbon content in MCM111 was 7.29% by weight versus 8.96% in NFPP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The slight difference in carbon content between the two samples indicates that it will result in an equivalent enhancement on conductivity. Additionally, Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) shows a similar I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> intensity ratio of  1.00, implying that the carbon skeletons in both MCM111 and NFPP are predominantly amorphous, with defects and vacancies present.</p>
            
            <p>For further details, HRTEM revealed clear lattice fringes within the MCM111 particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1e</a>) with a spacing of 0.472&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (211) crystalline planes of the NFPP. Additionally, a uniform carbon layer 7&nbsp;nm thick was observed on the sample surface. Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping showed that various elements were well distributed within MCM111 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1f</a>), indicating the successful incorporation of all elements into the bulk phase.</p>
            
            <p>Meanwhile, the accurate metal elemental content of Na, Fe, Cu, V, Mg, and Mn in MCM111 was quantified using inductively coupled plasma spectroscopy (ICP, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1g</a>), closely matched the pre-designed theoretical stoichiometry (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1a</a>). Similarly, NFPP also possessed a comparable surface carbon layer, and EDS elemental mapping indicated that Na, Fe, P, O, and C were distributed within the particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202412730-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>As mentioned earlier, adjusting the configuration entropy of electrode materials can significantly alter ion and electron transport efficiency, as well as electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0012_R_d132592361e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) test was conducted on the ESO samples and NFPP at 0.1C between 1.54.2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, as seen in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a> (Supporting Information). All the ESO samples exhibited higher specific capacities compared to NFPP, which had a specific capacity of only 97.7 mAh g<sup>1</sup>. Specifically, MCM111 displayed a specific capacity of 122.3 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, MCM133 of 100.5 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, MCM233 of 106.1 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, and MCM333 of 112.7 mAh g<sup>1</sup>. Notably, these ESO samples all demonstrated a longer and more pronounced voltage plateau, which was the main factor contributing to the increased capacities.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202412730-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ad4bdc8-329a-4dc2-a35a-88b1982cd048/adfm202412730-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ad4bdc8-329a-4dc2-a35a-88b1982cd048/adfm202412730-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f3b2c204-6c66-46d6-b219-029638c9ec19/adfm202412730-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ad4bdc8-329a-4dc2-a35a-88b1982cd048/adfm202412730-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202412730-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202412730" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of MCM111 and NFPP. a) Second charge/discharge curve at 0.1C between 1.54.2&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na); b) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup>; c) rate capability at various current rates from 0.5C to 100C; the cycling performances at 1C, 5C, and 50C df).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Following this, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed on these ESO samples and NFPP at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>1</sup> within the voltage range of 1.54.2&nbsp;V (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). It can be observed that all samples exhibit similar multiple oxidation/reduction peaks, implying the stepwise insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> in these materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0006_R_d132592361e1184" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Eng. J.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it is evident that the oxidation/reduction potential difference of MCM111 (0.24&nbsp;V) is visibly smaller than that of NFPP (0.33&nbsp;V). This decreased potential difference implied a reduction in electrochemical polarization during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process, suggesting favorable diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> compared to NFPP.</p>
            
            <p>The rate capability of MCM111 and NFPP were then evaluated from 0.5C to 100C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2c</a>) and the GCD curves were illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). It is notable that MCM111 exhibited a reversible capacity of 108.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at 0.5C and achieved 60.3 mAh g<sup>1</sup> even at an ultra-high rate of 100C. Upon returning to 0.5C, MCM111 was capable of fully recovering its initial capacity. However, NFPP released only 96.4 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.5C and 26.4 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 100C. Moreover, the corresponding GCD curves of the MCM111 also showed a reduced overpotential, especially at high rates. The impressive rate capability of MCM111 indicates that the ESO electrode materials efficiently and stably accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction.</p>
            
            <p>The cycling stabilities are investigated at different rates of 1C, 5C, and 50 C as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2df</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). MCM111 exhibits a reversible capacity of 104.2 mAh g<sup></sup> at 1C, with a capacity retention rate of 98.2% after 100 cycles, demonstrating excellent cycling stability at a low current rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2d</a>). In contrast, NFPP exhibits an initial capacity of only 90.1 mAh g<sup>1</sup> and a capacity retention rate of 88.7%. Similarly, the capacity retention of the other ESO samples exceeds 90% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information), all surpassing that of NFPP at 1C.</p>
            
            <p>Most notably, MCM111 maintains 96.0% of its initial capacity (90.2 mAh g<sup>1</sup>) after 500 cycles at 5C, which is substantially higher than the 65.5% retention rate and 82.1 mAh g<sup>1</sup> initial capacity of NFPP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2e</a>). More impressively, the MCM111 has an initial capacity of 76.7 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at an ultra-high rate of 50C and achieves a capacity retention rate of 78.38% after 14000 cycles, with a capacity degradation rate of only 0.0015% per cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2f</a>). This performance significantly surpasses that of NFPP, which exhibits a capacity retention rate of 46.27% in the same case.</p>
            
            <p>It is evident that compared to the current NFPP and previous similar materials, the ESO-modified samples demonstrated substantial enhancements in reversible capacity, cycling stability, and rate capability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b,c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the assembled hard carbonMCM111 full cell exhibits a discharge capacity of  97.1 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.1C, demonstrating its potential application (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202412730-sec-0050-title">2.3 Dynamics and Structure Stability</h3>
            
            <p>Based on the aforementioned electrochemical performance and discussion, it is clear that the rate performance and the cycling stability of the ESO-modified sodium ions and the structural stability of the material. Subsequently, the kinetic behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction within the sample was evaluated using cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT).</p>
            
            <p><b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a> (Supporting Information) display the CV scans on the MCM111 and NFPP from 0.2 to 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>1</sup> investigating the kinetic properties of Na<sup>+</sup>, and in both samples, the difference between the redox peaks increases significantly with the scan rate, indicating a typical behavior during the sodium insertion/extraction process. Furthermore, a linear correlation between the square root of the scan rate (<sup>1/2</sup>) and the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) was calculated (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating that the process is predominantly diffusion-controlled.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202412730-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/40696adf-beda-4287-bd0b-2af88a9a9e6e/adfm202412730-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/40696adf-beda-4287-bd0b-2af88a9a9e6e/adfm202412730-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d7724d4c-c20c-4475-b894-1f1933883a7e/adfm202412730-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/40696adf-beda-4287-bd0b-2af88a9a9e6e/adfm202412730-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202412730-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202412730" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at different rates of MCM111; b) linear relationship between the square root of the scanning rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) and the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) of MCM111; c) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during discharging of MCM111 and NFPP; d) in situ XRD contour pattern and e) stacked line plots of MCM111; f) pattern of variation of cell parameters of MCM111; and g) volumetric changes of MCM111 with other polyanionic compound materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can be determined by the Randles-Sevcik equation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0008_R_d132592361e1337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> specifically, the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> in MCM111 for peaks A1, A2, C1 and C2 are 1.12  10<sup>11</sup>, 2.33  10<sup>11</sup>, 1.20  10<sup>11</sup>, and 1.12  10<sup>11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup>, respectively. Nevertheless, NFPP shows lower D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values for the redox peaks of A1, A2, C1, and C2 (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The excellent diffusion kinetics of the MCM samples are also confirmed by the GITT measurements (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The high-entropy strategy not only accelerated the diffusion behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> but also enhanced the electronic conductivity of the material, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> (Supporting Information). Conductivity tests of the powders indicated that MCM111 exhibits significantly larger electronic conductivity compared to NFPP, which likely facilitated rapid electron transfer and improved rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2c</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and the corresponding fitting equivalent circuit model further support these arguments (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating that MCM111 exhibits lower charge transfer resistance and superior ionic conductivity. The increase in the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and electronic conductivity further confirms the effectiveness of the high-entropy strategy in enhancing the ion and electron transport efficiency of electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0014_R_d132592361e1397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>We also considered whether the rapid insertion/extraction of Na would affect the structural stability of MCM111. Therefore, the variation in crystal structure during the charge/discharge process was further elucidated through in situ XRD analysis. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3d</a> presents the in situ XRD contour map and the GCD curve for MCM111 at 0.1C, along with a representative stacking diagram depicted in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3e</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>During the charging process, the diffraction peaks of the (410), (402), (203), (022), and (420) crystal planes exhibit an obvious shift toward higher angles, signifying a contraction of the lattice during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. Notably, the peaks of the (013), (222), and (004) crystal planes initially shift to lower angles within the voltage range of 1.53.2&nbsp;V, while they shift to higher angles at voltages exceeding 3.2&nbsp;V. This is primarily due to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from the Na1 and Na4 sites around [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] at 3.2&nbsp;V, leading to localized rearrangement and deformation of the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub><sup>2</sup> moieties,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0023_R_d132592361e1431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> of which also was the main reasons of the irregular variations in cell parameters (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3f</a>).</p>
            
            <p>However, in the subsequent discharge stage, all peaks returned to their original positions, confirming the high structural reversibility of MCM111. Importantly, peak shifts only occurred during the charge/discharge process, with no new peaks appearing or existing peaks disappearing, implying that the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> storage process is a single-phase reaction, consistent with previous studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0004_R_d132592361e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0007_R_d132592361e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct Materials.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0008_R_d132592361e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the localized rearrangement and deformation of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub><sup>2</sup>, MCM111 exhibits a volume change rate of only 1.9% during full charge/discharge cycles, which is dramatically lower than that of other polyanion cathode materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3g</a>; Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4,S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202412730-sec-0060-title">2.4 Charge Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To further elucidate the charge storage mechanism during the insertion/extraction of Na <sup>+</sup> ions in MCM111 and NFPP cathode materials, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) shows the signals of all elements present in NFPP, with the observed binding energies of the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks located at 711.1 and 724.6&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating the presence of Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the original MCM111. Additional signals corresponding to the four doped elements (Mn, V, Cu, and Mg) were detected (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), providing evidence of the successful incorporation of the designed metal ions into the central Fe atom of the NFPP lattice structure.</p>
            
            <p>Next, we investigated whether these doped metal elements participate in the electrochemical reactions of MCM111. For further details, ex-situ XPS was utilized to detect the valence state changes of various elements in MCM111 at different potentials, aiming to elucidate the charge compensation mechanism. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0004">4a</a> illustrates that the initial state of the Fe in MCM111 is Fe<sup>2+</sup>. Upon charging to 2.9&nbsp;V, the binding energy of the Fe 2p orbitals exhibited a slight enhancement, indicating that Fe<sup>2+</sup> was beginning to be oxidized to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. As charging continued to 3.1&nbsp;V and eventually to 4.2&nbsp;V, Fe<sup>2+</sup> was fully oxidized to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. During the discharge cycle, Fe<sup>3+</sup> underwent gradual reduction, ultimately reaching Fe<sup>2+</sup> at 1.5&nbsp;V, thereby returning to its initial state.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202412730-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/952e59a2-4371-47d2-86db-5c82168d7d94/adfm202412730-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/952e59a2-4371-47d2-86db-5c82168d7d94/adfm202412730-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/957f04a7-1aa1-4af8-9133-31bf7275a037/adfm202412730-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/952e59a2-4371-47d2-86db-5c82168d7d94/adfm202412730-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202412730-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202412730" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">ac) Ex-situ XPS of Fe, Mn, and V during charging and discharging of MCM111; d) thumbnail sketch of crystal structure changes during charging of MCM111 and NFPP and schematic diagram of charge compensation mechanism.; e) schematic diagram of crystal structure changes during charging/discharging of MCM111.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Regarding Mn, the XPS spectrum initially showed binding energies of 641.1&nbsp;eV for 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 651.3&nbsp;eV for 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, corresponding to the Mn<sup>2+</sup> state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0004">4b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0003_R_d132592361e1540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the charging voltage increased, the binding energies of the Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> spin-orbit split orbitals showed a slight augmentation, indicative of the oxidation of Mn<sup>2</sup>. Upon completion of the charging process at 4.2&nbsp;V, all manganese ions were present in the Mn<sup>3</sup> state. This oxidation process is fully reversible, with the manganese ions being reduced to their original Mn<sup>2+</sup>state upon discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <p>Similarly, the XPS analysis of V 2p orbitals exhibited periodic changes during charging/discharging processes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0004">4c</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0003_R_d132592361e1562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with V<sup>4+</sup> in the fully charged state at 4.2&nbsp;V and V<sup>3+</sup> in the fully discharged state of 1.5&nbsp;V. However, for the other two doping elements, Cu and Mg, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information), the binding energies of Cu 2p and Mg 1s remained unchanged throughout the entire charging/discharging cycle. This inert behavior suggests that Cu and Mg do not participate in electrochemical reactions in MCM111. Overall, the redox processes of Fe<sup>2</sup>/Fe<sup>3</sup>, Mn<sup>2</sup>/Mn<sup>3</sup>, and V<sup>3</sup>/V are the primary contributors to the charge compensation mechanism in MCM111.</p>
            
            <p>Based on the XPS analysis above, we can deduce the contribution of each element during the charging and discharging process, by correlating the charging curve at 0.1C with the active element at specific potentials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0004">4d</a>). According to the theoretical capacity of NFPP or MCM111, the extraction of one mole of Na<sup>+</sup> corresponds to the oxidation of one mole of active element, releasing a capacity of 43 mAh g<sup>1</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>Initially, at 2.9&nbsp;V, 0.6 moles of Fe<sup>2+</sup>, 0.01 of Mn<sup>2+</sup>, and 0.02 of V<sup>3+</sup> (based on MCM111) were oxidized, yielding a capacity of 27 mAh g. Upon increasing the voltage to 3.1&nbsp;V, additional oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, and V<sup>3+</sup> occurred generating 63 mAh g<sup>1</sup> of capacity. Finally, at 4.2&nbsp;V, Fe<sup>2</sup> was fully oxidized to Fe<sup>3</sup>, while Mn and V existed in the forms of Mn<sup>3</sup> and V, respectively, resulting in a capacity of 121 mAh g.</p>
            
            <p>Throughout this charge process, MCM111 achieved the extraction of 2.8 Na, demonstrating an impressive deep sodium insertion/extraction capability. In contrast, NFPP only achieved reversible deintercalation of 2.2 Na<sup>+</sup>. Therefore, owing to its charge compensation mechanism, MCM111 indeed demonstrated enhanced Na insertion/extraction capability, which explains the higher capacity observed in the GCD tests (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2a</a>).</p>
            
            <p>As is well known for electrode materials, the migration of Na ions within the crystal lattice induces changes in the valence states of the active elements, which inevitably impacts the crystal structure. The crystal structure of MCM11l is primarily comprised of three types of coordinated NaO polyhedral, Fe (Mn, V, Cu, Mg)O octahedra, PO tetrahedra, and [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] (with two PO tetrahedra sharing an oxygen atom to form). During the charging process, the release order of Na follows a structural evolution sequence, progressing from Na3 (5-coordination) to Na1 (6-coordination), and finally to Na4 (6-coordination).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0007_R_d132592361e1635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct Materials.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0024_R_d132592361e1635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - EcoMat.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>For MCM111, when the voltage increased from 1.52.9&nbsp;V, approximately six Na3 sites were be extracted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0004">4e</a>). Subsequently, another two Na3 sites and five Na1 sites were extracted from 2.9  3.1&nbsp;V. Finally, from 3.14.2&nbsp;V, an additional three Na1 sites and seven Na4 sites were extracted, accompanied by structural rearrangement of [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] during this stage. MCM111 retained one Na4 site and eight Na2 sites without any movement, whereas NFPP ultimately retained seven Na4 sites and eight Na2 sites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). The difference in the depth of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction behavior between the two samples also explains the longer and more pronounced discharge plateau observed for MCM111 in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2a</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202412730-sec-0070-title">2.5 Cathode-Electrolyte Interface</h3>
            
            <p>As previously discussed, the advantage of employing a high entropy strategy lies in its cumulative effect, where each doped element plays a unique role, commonly known as the cocktail effect, and significantly enhances the material's overall performance. XPS analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information) revealed that the binding energies of Cu and Mg remained unchanged, indicating that these elements did not participate in electrochemical reactions throughout the entire charge/discharge process. So, will they contribute to the interface stability of the material? Numerous studies have found that a good cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) can indeed enhance the electrochemical performance of electrode materials, such as MgO<sup></sup>, LiMgCl<sub>4</sub><sup></sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0025_R_d132592361e1675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Soc. Rev. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) was conducted on the MCM111 after charge/discharge cycling to detect its cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5</a> revealed the presence of numerous organic ions and metal oxide ion fragments, which were primarily formed through the interaction between the electrolyte and MCM111 during the charge/discharge process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202412730-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2f56e97-5ff4-41db-859f-d4777fda8384/adfm202412730-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2f56e97-5ff4-41db-859f-d4777fda8384/adfm202412730-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bf86fc7b-c89b-41d4-8d26-f5b5abc1c820/adfm202412730-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2f56e97-5ff4-41db-859f-d4777fda8384/adfm202412730-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202412730-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202412730" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TOF-SIMS secondary ion images of the anion depth profile (ADP) 3D images (upper) and corresponding anion ion mapping (AIM) images (lower) of the major ionic fragments a) and the supplementary ionic fragments b); c) the depth analysis curves of supplementary ionic fragments; d) schematic diagram of the possible roles of the oxide layer species.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>For the anion depth profile (ADP), the 3D image presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5a</a> (upper), shows a distinct surface layering formed by various C, P, Na, and Fe ion fragments, confirming the existence of a CEI layer. Notably, this CEI layer appears incomplete and fragmented, as illustrated in the anion ion mapping (AIM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5a</a> (lower). Surprisingly, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5b</a>, the AIM image reveals that various MnO<sup></sup>, MgO<sup></sup>, VO<sup></sup>, and CuO<sup></sup> ion fragments completely cover the CEI layer, creating a robust complementary effect with the fragmented CEI layer depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5a</a>. Ultimately, an almost perfect CEI film formed on the MCM111 surface, which contrasts sharply with the incomplete CEI in NFPP. More details of some other anionic fragments are provided in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Further observations indicate that the signal intensity of MgO<sup></sup> initially increased sharply but gradually decreased over time (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5c</a>). In contrast, the signal intensities of MnO<sup></sup>, VO<sup></sup>, and CuO<sup></sup> progressively increased, suggesting that MgO<sup></sup> fragments were predominantly located on the surface of the CEI, while the other oxides were distributed internally. The TOF-SIMS results provide strong evidence that employing a high-entropy strategy, along with the selection of appropriate doping elements, can indeed facilitate the formation of a CEI protective layer composed of various metal oxides (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0005">5d</a>). This protective layer performs multiple functions: it mitigates the corrosive effects of electrolytes on electrode materials, suppresses side reactions during electrochemical processes, and prevents the dissolution and dispersion of metal elements from the bulk material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0026_R_d132592361e1755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Z. Lin, Energy Environ. Sci. Nat Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This preservation of structural integrity is likely a key factor contributing to the excellent cycling performance of MCM111 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2df</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202412730-sec-0080-title">2.6 DFT Calculations and Na<sup>+</sup> Migration Path</h3>
            
            <p>Experimental results have demonstrated that the entropy strategy is indeed an effective means to improve the conductivity, ion transport property, and structural stability of the material. To gain a deeper understanding of the intrinsic characteristics of the cocktail effect, detailed density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0006">6a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) display the theoretical crystal model of MCM111 and the corresponding three-view projections, facilitating multi-angle visualization of the elemental occupancy. In this model, the <i>b</i>-axis and <i>c</i>-axis in MCM111 were expanded relative to NFPP, while the <i>a</i>-axis was slightly contracted. This result of the theoretical calculations is quite consistent with the previously refined data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0001">1d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), thereby confirming the feasibility of the proposed theoretical crystal model.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202412730-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ffa0ad90-0036-4c25-ac11-5815bf8adb92/adfm202412730-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ffa0ad90-0036-4c25-ac11-5815bf8adb92/adfm202412730-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1f6ff8b-49c3-4756-a929-b21d070c0db4/adfm202412730-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ffa0ad90-0036-4c25-ac11-5815bf8adb92/adfm202412730-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202412730-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202412730" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculations and analysis of NFPP and MCM111. a) Theoretical crystal structures; b,c) TDOS and pDOS patterns; schematic representation of charge density distribution d,e) the red regions denote electron accumulation, and the blue regions indicate electron dispersion) and differential charge f,g) electron accumulation is yellow and electron depletion is blue); migration pathways with corresponding migration energy barriers h,i).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on this model, the total density of states (tDOS) and partial density of states (pDOS) of various elements in MCM111 and NFPP were calculated and presented in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0006">6b,c</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information). Compared to the band gap value of 2.97&nbsp;eV in NFPP, MCM111 showed a significant reduction to 0.86&nbsp;eV. The narrowing of the band gap predominantly occurred on the conduction band side, driven by the transition of Fe 3d electrons in the conduction band of MCM111 to lower energy states, facilitating easier electron movement from the valence band to the conduction band (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26b</a>, Supporting Information). However, on the valence band side, the Fe 3d orbitals in MCM111 became less favorable for electron transition. Coincidentally, the pDOS of the doped elements of Mn, V, and Cu effectively compensated for the missing energy bands, indicating that electrons associated with Fe can transition from the valence band to the conduction band via the doped elements, thereby enhancing electron transfer capability. Importantly, the maximum value of the V valence band crossed the Fermi level (0&nbsp;eV), which is likely the key factor in improving the electronic conductivity of the MCM111 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, the calculations of charge density distribution and charge density difference visually illustrated the variations in electron cloud density around various elements. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0006">6d,e</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information) display the electron localization function (ELF) projections of various elements in MCM111 compared to Fe in NFPP, showing the distribution of electrons in real space. Compared to FeO bonds, MnO, CuO, and VO bonds exhibited more red and green coloration, indicating an increased degree of electron localization due to doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0027_R_d132592361e1839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, the absence of localized electrons around MgO bonds suggests that MgO bonds are inherently more ionic in nature, which typically results in higher bond energy compared to covalent bonds (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the charge density difference diagram (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0006">6f,g</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information) reveals that compared to the Fe element in the same position in NFPP, there was a greater electron loss around the doped elements in MCM111, demonstrating that the bonding interaction between O atoms and the doped elements is stronger than the interaction between O and Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202412730-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202412730-bib-0028_R_d132592361e1854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Small Struct. Carbon Neutralization">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enhancement of these chemical bonds allows MCM111 to maintain structural stability even after large amounts of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction, which is another contributing factor to its excellent cycling performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2df</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Variations in electron cloud density not only impact structural stability but also exert a profound influence on the energy barriers to Na<sup>+</sup> migration. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0006">6h,i</a> depict the migration energy barriers of MCM111 and NFPP with the same sodium ion migration path. Remarkably, the migration barrier for MCM111 was 0.502&nbsp;eV, which is lower than the 0.655&nbsp;eV barrier observed for NFPP along the same pathway. This lower barrier implies that the ESO sample significantly facilitated deeper Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction, as corroborated by the GITT results (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, this reduced migration barrier directly contributes to the superior kinetic behavior of MCM111 (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0002">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202412730-fig-0003">3</a>).</p>
            
            <p>So far, DFT analysis has demonstrated in theory that the entropy strategy can effectively reduce band gaps, alter electron cloud density, and lower migration barriers in electrode materials. By selecting appropriate doping elements, this strategy further enhances electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion capability, which allows the excellent reaction kinetics, structural stability, and electrochemical performance of MCM111, validating the fundamental principle of the high entropy strategy, known as the cocktail effect.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202412730-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202412730-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we employed a high entropy strategy to design and synthesize MCM111 as the cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. This material exhibits exceptional electrochemical performance due to its stable structure (with a volume change of only 1.9%) and deep insertion/extraction capabilities (reversible insertion/extraction of 2.8 Na<sup>+</sup>). Specifically, it delivers a reversible capacity of 122.3 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.1C, maintains a rate capacity of 60.3 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 100C, and boasts an ultra-long cycle life of 14000 cycles at 50C, with a capacity loss of only 0.0015% per cycle. Furthermore, in situ and ex-situ techniques have demonstrated that Fe, Mn, and V play pivotal roles in promoting the charge compensation mechanism during deep Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, whereas Mg and Cu participate in the formation of a stable CEI film. Additionally, DFT calculations reveal that the enhancement of transition metal-oxygen bonds is crucial for structural stability, the reduction of the bandgap increases electronic conductivity, and the lower migration energy barrier facilitates rapid Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. This study provides a new perspective on the cocktail effect by examining charge compensation mechanisms, interface stability, and electron cloud density, offering deeper insights into the high entropy effect.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
